class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"><div class="aLW4J" track-metadata-module="left rail"><h5 class="qZDXV Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-wNfPc">Mandiant Incident Response</h5><p class="nRhiJb-cHYyed Mrfygc nRhiJb-BP2Omd-ma6Yeb-KE6vqe nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-cGMI2b">Investigate, contain, and remediate security incidents.</p><a class="nRhiJb-LgbsSe  nRhiJb-LgbsSe-OWXEXe-CNusmb-o6Shpd " href="https://cloud.google.com/security/consulting/mandiant-incident-response-services" track-metadata-module="left rail"track-metadata-module_headline="mandiant incident response" track-name="learn more"track-type="button"track-metadata-position="cta"track-metadata-eventdetail="cloud.google.com/security/consulting/mandiant-incident-response-services">Learn more </a></div></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Lukasz Lamparski, Punsaen Boonyakarn, Shawn Chew, Frank Tse, Jakub Jozwiak, Mathew Potaczek, Logeswaran Nadarajan, Nick Harbour, Mustafa Nasser</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Introduction</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">In mid 2024, Mandiant discovered threat actors deployed custom backdoors on Juniper Networks’ Junos OS routers. Mandiant attributed these backdoors to the China-nexus espionage group, UNC3886. Mandiant uncovered several TINYSHELL-based backdoors operating on Juniper Networks’ Junos OS routers. The backdoors had varying custom capabilities, including active and passive backdoor functions, as well as an embedded script that disables logging mechanisms on the target device.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant worked with Juniper Networks to investigate this activity and observed that the affected Juniper MX routers were running end-of-life hardware and software. Mandiant recommends that organizations upgrade their Juniper devices to the latest images </span><a href="https://supportportal.juniper.net/JSA93446" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">released</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> by Juniper Networks, which includes mitigations and updated signatures for the </span><a href="https://www.juniper.net/documentation/us/en/software/junos/security-services/topics/concept/juniper-malware-removal-tool.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Juniper Malware Removal Tool</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> (JMRT). Organizations should run the JMRT Quick Scan and Integrity Check after the upgrade. <span style="vertical-align:baseline">Juniper also </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">released an </span><a href="https://supportportal.juniper.net/s/article/2025-03-Reference-Advisory-The-RedPenguin-Malware-Incident?language=en_US" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">advisory</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> about this incident.</span></span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant has reported on similar custom malware ecosystems in 2022 and 2023 that UNC3886 deployed on </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/esxi-hypervisors-malware-persistence"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">virtualization technologies</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/fortinet-malware-ecosystem"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">network edge devices</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. This blog post showcases a development in UNC3886’s tactics, techniques and procedures (TTPs), and their focus on malware and capabilities that enable them to operate on network and edge devices, which typically lack security monitoring and detection solutions, such as endpoint detection and response (EDR) agents. </span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant previously reported on UNC3886's emphasis on techniques to gather and use</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/uncovering-unc3886-espionage-operations"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">legitimate credentials</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to move laterally within a network, undetected. These objectives remained consistent but were pursued with the introduction of a new tool in 2024. Observations in this blog post strengthen our assessment that the actor’s focus is on maintaining long-term access to victim networks. UNC3886 continues to show a deep understanding of the underlying technology of the appliances being targeted.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">At the time of writing, Mandiant has not identified any technical overlaps between activities detailed in this blog post and those publicly reported by other parties as Volt Typhoon or Salt Typhoon. </span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Attribution</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">UNC3886 is a highly adept China-nexus cyber espionage group that has historically targeted network devices and virtualization technologies with zero-day exploits. UNC3886 interests seem to be focused mainly on defense, technology, and telecommunication organizations located in the US and Asia. The activity described in this blog post is the latest in a number of operations where UNC3886 has leveraged custom malware to target network devices. The malware deployed on Juniper Networks’ Junos OS routers demonstrates that UNC3886 has in-depth knowledge of advanced system internals. Furthermore, UNC3886 continues to prioritize stealth in its operations through the use of passive backdoors, together with log and forensics artifact tampering, indicating a focus on long-term persistence, while minimizing the risk of detection.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Junos OS</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Juniper Networks </span><a href="https://www.juniper.net/documentation/us/en/software/junos/junos-overview/topics/concept/junos-software-introduction.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Junos OS</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is a proprietary operating system that powers most Juniper routing, switching, and security devices. It is based on a modified FreeBSD operating system. Junos OS supports 2 different modes of operations:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">CLI mode:</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> where standard Junos OS CLI commands can be issued</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Shell mode:</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> a user with shell access privileges can access an underlying FreeBSD shell and issue standard FreeBSD commands.</span></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Malware identified in this blog post primarily relies on access to the csh shell, but in some cases it is also aware of higher layers.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Veriexec</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Junos OS incorporates a </span><a href="https://www.juniper.net/documentation/us/en/software/junos/junos-install-upgrade/topics/concept/veriexec.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Verified Exec (veriexec)</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> subsystem, which is a modified version of an original </span><a href="https://www.netbsd.org/docs/guide/en/chap-veriexec.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">NetBSD Veriexec Subsystem</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. Veriexec is a kernel-based file integrity subsystem that protects the Junos OS operating system (OS) against unauthorized code including binaries, libraries, and scripts and activity that might compromise the integrity of the device. To run malware, the threat actor first needed to bypass veriexec protection.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant did not observe evidence indicating successful exploitation of veriexec bypass techniques already addressed by Juniper in supported software and hardware. However, aside from the process injection technique described later in this blog post, infection on the compromised EOL Juniper MX routers indicate that the threat actor successfully deployed executable backdoors. Mandiant identified the threat actor had root access to the impacted devices.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Circumventing Veriexec with Process Injection</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Veriexec protection prevents unauthorized binaries from executing. This poses a challenge for threat actors, as disabling veriexec can trigger alerts. However, execution of untrusted code is still possible if it occurs within the context of a trusted process. Mandiant’s investigation revealed that UNC3886 was able to circumvent this protection by injecting malicious code into the memory of a legitimate process. <span style="vertical-align:baseline">This specific technique is now tracked as CVE-2025-21590, as detailed in Juniper Network’s security bulletin </span><a href="https://supportportal.juniper.net/JSA93446" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">JSA93446</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">To achieve this, UNC3886 first gained privileged access to a Juniper router from a terminal server used for managing network devices using legitimate credentials, and entered the FreeBSD shell from the Junos OS CLI. Within the shell environment, they used </span><a href="https://tldp.org/LDP/abs/html/here-docs.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">the “here document” feature</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to generate a base64 encoded file named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">ldb.b64</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. This encoded file was then decoded using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">base64</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to create a compressed archive named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">ldb.tar.gz</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, which was subsequently decompressed and extracted using the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">gunzip</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">tar</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> utilities to extract malicious binaries.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant was unable to recover the full content of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">ldb.b64</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> or </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">ldb.tar.gz</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> on the compromised Juniper routers' file system. However, Mandiant successfully recovered three malicious payloads by performing analysis on the memory of a compromised router. The purpose of the payloads was as follows:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">loader.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is a shellcode loader responsible for loading functions including </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">exit</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">mmap</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">open</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">read</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, and </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">close</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> from a standard library </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">libc.so.7</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, allocating memory, and loading and executing the final payload from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">payload.bin</code></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pc.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> contains a memory address </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x4012f0</code></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">payload.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> was identified to be the Position Independent Code (PIC) version of the lmpad backdoor</span></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Details of lmpad backdoor are covered in the Malware Analysis section.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant observed the threat actor inject malicious payloads into a newly spawned </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">cat</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> process. The actor created a named pipe called </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">null</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">mkfifo</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and used </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">cat</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to continuously read from it, effectively creating a hung process. This stage involved the following commands:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>rm -rf null;
mkfifo null;
cat null &amp;
set pid=$!
echo &quot; $pid&quot;</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">While the hung </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">cat</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> process was waiting for data from the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">null</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> pipe, the threat actor leveraged </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">dd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to read binary data from the payload files and write it to specific memory locations inside the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">cat</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> process.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>dd if=loader.bin of=/proc/$pid/mem conv=notrunc obs=1 oseek=0x4012f0
dd if=pc.bin of=/proc/$pid/mem conv=notrunc obs=1 oseek=0x602820</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The first </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">dd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> command wrote the loader code from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">loader.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> at the virtual address </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x4012f0,</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> which is the entrypoint of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">cat</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The second </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">dd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> command replaced data at </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x602820</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> with the content of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pc.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. Mandiant noted that </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x602820</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is the global offset table entry for </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">fclose</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, this memory location was overwritten with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x4012f0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, indicating that </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pc.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> contains the memory address where </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">loader.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> was injected to.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The threat actor sent an empty string using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">echo</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">null</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> pipe. </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">cat</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> received an end-of-file signal after </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">echo</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> finished writing the data and attempted to close the file by executing </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">fclose</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">As the global offset table entry for </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">fclose</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> function was replaced with the entrypoint to the shellcode loader, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">cat</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> executed the shellcode loader instead of the actual </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">fclose</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> function, and ultimately loaded the final payload from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">payload.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> in the same directory.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">After </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">payload.bin</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> has been loaded, the threat actor removed the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">null</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> file and the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">ldb</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> directory, then terminated the current session. This left only the legitimate process running on the compromised router, now containing the malicious code. The following commands were used to achieve these actions:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>sleep 1;echo -n&gt;null;sleep 1;rm -rf null
cd ..
rm -rf ldb	
kill -9 $$</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant’s investigation noted that this process injection is intended for executing the PIC version of the lmpad backdoor while veriexec is enabled and does not support execution of other backdoors identified on the file system of the compromised Juniper routers. </span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Malware Overview</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant’s investigation identified six distinct malware samples across multiple Juniper MX routers. Each sample is a modified version of a TINYSHELL backdoor, but with unique capabilities. All of these samples incorporate a core TINYSHELL backdoor functionality, but differ greatly when it comes to activation methods as well as additional, Junos OS specific features.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The following malware samples were identified:</span></p>
<ol>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">appid</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - TINYSHELL-based active backdoor, mimicking a legitimate binary named appidd (Application Identification Daemon)</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">to</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - TINYSHELL-based active backdoor, mimicking a legitimate binary named top (Table of Processes)</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">irad</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - TINYSHELL-based passive backdoor, mimicking a legitimate binary named irsd (Interface Replication and Synchronization Daemon)</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">lmpad</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - TINYSHELL-based utility and passive backdoor, mimicking a legitimate binary named lmpd (Link Management Protocol Daemon)</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">jdosd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - TINYSHELL-based passive backdoor, mimicking a legitimate binary named jddosd (Juniper DDOS protection Daemon)</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">oemd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - TINYSHELL-based passive backdoor, mimicking a legitimate binary named oamd (Operation, Administration and Maintenance Daemon)</span></p>
</li>
</ol>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">TINYSHELL</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">TINYSHELL is a </span><a href="https://github.com/creaktive/tsh" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">publicly available</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> lightweight backdoor written in C that communicates using a custom binary protocol. The standard set of TINYSHELL commands comprises of:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Remote file upload</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Remote file download</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Establishing remote shell session</span></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">A basic TINYSHELL implementation for FreeBSD seems to be a foundation for heavily customized backdoors detailed as follows.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Malware Analysis</span></h2>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">appid — TINYSHELL-Based Active Backdoor</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Sample one, named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">appid</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, is an active backdoor written in C. It is derived from the publicly available TINYSHELL source code with additional supported commands. It is an active backdoor that communicates to the following hardcoded command and control (C2) servers:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">TCP://129[.]126[.]109[.]50:22</code></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">TCP://116[.]88[.]34[.]184:22</code></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">TCP://223[.]25[.]78[.]136:22</code></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">TCP://45[.]77[.]39[.]28:22</code></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant believes these IPs are staging nodes of a GOBRAT </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/china-nexus-espionage-orb-networks"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">ORB network</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, eventually leading to a single, backend Adversary Controlled Operations Server (“ACOS”).</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This malware begins by communicating to a random C2 server from the list. The malware maintains two TCP sockets that will stay synchronized with the same C2 address. One socket is used for tasking requests and the other is for handling requests. The malware will rotate through the list of C2 servers until a successful connection is created and it will request a task using the first socket. After receiving a task from the C2, the malware then creates a second socket for handling this specific task. After the task is finished, the second socket is closed.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The malware encrypts all network traffic with AES using a hard-coded key. The following commands are supported by malware, consisting of standard TINYSHELL commands and added proxy and reconfiguration capabilities:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><div align="left">

<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">

</div></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">to — TINYSHELL-Based Active Backdoor </span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Sample two, named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">to</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, is the same as Sample 1 but with different hardcoded C2 servers:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">tcp://101[.]100[.]182[.]122:22</code></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">tcp://118[.]189[.]188[.]122:22</code></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">tcp://158[.]140[.]135[.]244:22</code></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">tcp://8[.]222[.]225[.]8:22</code></p>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">irad — TINYSHELL-Based Passive Backdoor</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Sample three, named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">irad</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, is a TINYSHELL-based passive backdoor written in C. It acts as a libpcap-based packet sniffer and receives commands by inspecting packets on the wire looking for a magic-string that activates its backdoor capabilities. Malware supports 2 modes of operation: active mode, in which it will connect to a provided C2 address, or a passive - listening mode.  In addition to 3 standard TINYSHELL commands, it implements 2 custom commands and a custom activation routine.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The malware uses libpcap library to capture all network packets on the host (interface specified in the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">eth</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> environment variable) matching the BPF filter of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">icmp[4:2] == 0xaa56</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. It reads 16 bytes of data starting at offset 10 from the ICMP packet. The malware has insufficient bounds checking and could read past the end of a packet payload if a smaller than expected packet is encountered.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">For any ICMP type code 8 (Echo request) packets, it reads the 16 bytes of data and decrypts it with a single byte XOR key </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x86.</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> It then compares the decrypted data with a magic string </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">uSarguuS62bKRA0J</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. If the string matches and the byte 8 of the ICMP packet matches ascii character </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">1</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> or </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">2</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, it then reads the target IP and TCP port number from the ICMP packet and sends a beacon packet containing string </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">WZtOTig2m42gXB6U</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to the specified IP (active mode). If the response from the target host begins with the string </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">ek63a21km7WSWkfk</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> then it exits, otherwise it decodes the command number from the packet:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><div align="left">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">

</div></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">If byte 8 of the ICMP packet is </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, the malware will spawn a listening server on TCP </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">port 31234</code> <span style="vertical-align:baseline">(passive mode). The listening backdoor server supports the same set of 5 commands as listed in the previous table.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">If the string </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">1spCq0BMbJwCoeZn</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is received, and the malware is currently operating as a listener and accepting connections on </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">port 31234</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, then it will terminate the listening process.</span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">lmpad — TINYSHELL-Based Utility and Passive Backdoor</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Sample four, named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">lmpad</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is an utility and a passive backdoor written in C. In addition to standard TINYSHELL capabilities it can also launch an external script that can perform process injection into two legitimate Junos OS processes to inhibit logging. The main purpose of this malware is to disable all possible logging before the operator connects to the router to perform hands-on activities and then later restore the logs after the operator disconnects. </span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The malware begins by attempting to receive a UDP packet on port 33615, where it receives an RC4 encrypted message and sends an acknowledgement message in response. The malware also checks the decrypted received payload against the following hex bytes, terminating the connection if it matches.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>26 e7 2b 3a 1c a2 16 2d 61 89 57 a9 cd 4c e7 3c</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The RC4 encryption key used to decrypt received UDP messages is: </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0b3330c0b41d1ae2</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">After the initial check, the malware enters a listening mode, waiting for commands. In addition to standard TINYSHELL commands (command 2, 4 and 5) it also implements 5 other custom commands:</span></p>
<h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0</code></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command inhibits logging in preparation of hands-on threat actor activity on the router. It begins by writing a compressed zip archive to the file </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. It then executes the following command line to decompress the archive, execute an embedded script with argument </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pre</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and clean up the script after execution:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>gzip -d /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log -c &gt; /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak; 
sh /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak pre ;
rm -rf /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak ;
kill -9 $$</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The following is the uncompressed script file:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>pre_ssh() {
#closelog
cp /mfs/var/etc/syslog.conf /mfs/var/etc/syslog.conf0
sed -i '' 's/\/dev\/null #//g' /mfs/var/etc/syslog.conf0
sed -i '' 's/        /        \/dev\/null #/g' /mfs/var/etc/syslog.conf
ps -fcA |grep eventd | awk '{ print $1 }' | xargs kill -1
#last
cp -r /var/log/utx.log /var/log/utx.log0
cp -r /var/log/wtmp /var/log/wtmp0
}

post_ssh() {
#relog
cp /mfs/var/etc/syslog.conf0 /mfs/var/etc/syslog.conf
rm -f /mfs/var/etc/syslog.conf0
ps -fcA | grep eventd | awk '{ print $1 }' | xargs kill -1
#relast
cp -r /var/log/wtmp0 /var/log/wtmp
cp -r /var/log/utx.log /var/log/utx.log0
rm -f /var/log/wtmp0
}

backup() {
#backconf
rm -rf /var/rundb+
cp -r /var/rundb /var/rundb+
cp /var/db/commits /usr/lib/libjucomm.so.1
tar -cf /config/usage_db /config/juniper.conf.*
tar -cf /var/db/config/usage_db /var/db/config/juniper.conf.*
}

restore() {
#reconfig
cp -r /var/rundb+/* /var/rundb
cp /usr/lib/libjucomm.so.1 /var/db/commits
tar -xf /config/usage_db -C /
tar -xf /var/db/config/usage_db -C /
rm -r /var/rundb+
rm -f /usr/lib/libjucomm.so.1
rm -f /config/usage_db
rm -f /var/db/config/usage_db
}

if [ $1 = "pre" ]; then
pre_ssh
elif [ $1 = "post" ]; then
post_ssh
elif [ $1 = "backup" ]; then
backup
elif [ $1 = "restore" ]; then
restore
fi

echo done
exit 0</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This script can execute 4 commands:</span></p>
<ol>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pre</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: backups </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">syslog.conf</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">syslog.conf0</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, it also redirects all logging to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/dev/null</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, sends HUP signal to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">eventd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> daemon responsible for logging and backs up last log and wtmp log. </span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">post</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: reverses changes done by </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pre_ssh</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, it restores original </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">syslog.conf</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as well as last log and wtmp log, it removes backups and sends another HUP signal to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">eventd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> process. </span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">backup</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: it backups the current Juniper configuration database and commit logs and archives configuration files. </span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:decimal;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">restore</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: it restores previously backed up configuration database and config files and removes backups. </span></p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">After stopping logging with </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">pre_ssh</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> script, this command then reads the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">snmpd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> process PID from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/run/snmpd.pid</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. It also reads 4 bytes of data from address </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x8601328</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and writes it to the file </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/tmp/rts</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> by using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">dd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>dd if=/proc/&lt;pid&gt;/mem of=/var/tmp/rts bs=1 count=4 iseek=0x8601328 2&gt;/dev/null</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">It then changes this data to 0 and writes it to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/tmp/rts</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and later back to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">snmpd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> process memory using the same </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">dd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> technique:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>dd of=/proc/&lt;pid&gt;/mem if=/var/tmp/rts bs=1 count=4 oseek=0x8601328 
conv=notrunc 2&gt;/dev/null</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This patches the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">snmpd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> process memory at address  </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x8601328</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The patch sets a global variable named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">trap_configs</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to 0. </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">trap_configs</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> possibly points to a structure containing SNMP traps that are sent to the monitoring server. Effectively this stops SNMP traps from being triggered which is most likely done to stop SNMP traps from triggering when specific events stop being logged in syslog. </span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The malware then repeats similar actions against the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">mgd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> (Juniper Management Daemon) process. It reads </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">mgd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> process PID from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/run/mgd.pid</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and using the same technique previously mentioned it reads 4 bytes from  </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">mgd</code> <span style="vertical-align:baseline">process memory at </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x84E90D8</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> . It compares these bytes to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">57E58955</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> (which after swapping endianness, disassembles to: </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">PUSH RBP; MOV EBP,ESP; PUSH RDI</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> instructions which is a typical function prologue), and if they match, it patches them with following bytes: </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">C3D08990</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. If we disassemble these bytes from a little endian order, we get </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">NOP; MOV EAX,EDX; RET</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> instructions.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This patch modifies the prologue of the function at </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0x84E90D8</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">NOP, MOV EAX,EDX, RET</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">  effectively skipping the rest of the function. Based on the behavioral analysis, the goal of this patch is likely to stop </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">auditd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> logging of MGD login, logout, cmd events. </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> </span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This direct memory manipulation using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">dd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> allows the attacker to alter program behavior and bypass security measures. UNC3886 previously used a similar tactic, where </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">dd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> was used to </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/fortinet-malware-ecosystem#:~:text=Disabling%20File%20System%20Verification%20on%20Startup"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">disable file system verification on startup</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. In that case, UNC3886 exploited a vulnerability (CVE-2022-41328) to overwrite legitimate FortiOS system binaries, achieving persistence and evading security checks. </span></p>
<h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">1</code></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command can reverse operations performed by Command 0 after the operator finished their hands-on-keyboard operations. It restores logging by executing </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sh /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> post, it also reverses patches to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">snmpd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">mgd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">2</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> — Launch CSH Session</span></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command launches an interactive </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/bin/csh</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> session over the UDP connection, with a forked process encrypting the input and output of the shell and transmitting it over the network.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Before executing remote shell, this commands launches a series of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">sed</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> commands to remove specific lines from log files:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>sed -i '' '/root/d' /var/log/interactive-commands
sed -i '' -e '/vi/d' -e '/set/d' -e '/gdb/d' -e '/mgd/d' /root/.history
sed -i '' '/root/d' /var/log/messages
sed -i '' '/root/d' /var/log/auth</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">These remove lines containing the term </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">root</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/log/interactive-commands</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/log/messages</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, and </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/var/log/auth</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. They also remove instances of </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">vi</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">set</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">gdb</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, and </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">mgd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> from </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">/root/.history</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
<h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">3</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> — Request New Sleep Timeout</span></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command will cause the malware to send a reply to the connection containing the value of the current sleep timeout. The response to that message will contain the new 4-byte sleep timeout that will be used by the main command loop (in seconds).</span></p>
<h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">4</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> — File Upload</span></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command will cause the malware to send a specified file to the remote server.</span></p>
<h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">5</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> — File Download</span></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command will cause the malware to download a file from the remote server and write it to the disk.</span></p>
<h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">6</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> — Backup Configuration Database</span></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command causes the malware to execute the same script as Command 0 but with argument </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">backup</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to backup Juniper configuration database and commit logs.</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>gzip -d /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log -c &gt; /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak; 
sh /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak backup ;
rm -rf /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak ;
kill -9 $$</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">7</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> — Restore configuration database</span></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command causes the malware to execute the same script as Command 0 but with argument </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">restore</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> to restore Juniper configuration database and commit logs:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>gzip -d /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log -c &gt; /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak; 
sh /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak restore ;
rm -rf /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log /var/tmp/pfed_jdhcp6_trace.log.bak ;
kill -9 $$</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h4><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">8</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> - Receive new socket timeout value</span></h4>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This command will cause the malware to send a reply to the connection containing the value of the current socket timeout. The response to that message will contain the new 4-byte value that will be used to update the main socket timeout. The default socket timeout value is 300 seconds</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">If any other command is passed, malware will close the socket and exit. </span></p>
<h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">jdosd — TINYSHELL-Based Passive Backdoor</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Sample five, named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">jdosd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, is a passive backdoor written in C. It implements a UDP backdoor operating on a fixed port number which provides file transfer and remote shell capabilities.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Malware binds to UDP port 33512 and uses a custom RC4 implementation. This implementation has a bug in it where it doesn't properly retrieve a final state box value during its PRGA generation. The following key is used for the traffic encryption:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>4fd37426-65dd-4a8d-8ba6-1382a011dae9</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The attacker initiates the connection to the backdoor by sending a magic value </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0xDEADBEEF</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. The malware responds to this message by sending the same message in response, encrypted with custom RC4. The malware will then send the process ID (pid) of its own process to the C2.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">After the initial beacon the malware waits for additional commands. In addition to standard TINYSHELL commands (1-3), there are two additional commands supported(0xAC, 0xFF):</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><div align="left">

</div>
</div></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h3><span style="vertical-align:baseline">oemd — TINYSHELL-Based Passive Backdoor</span></h3>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Sample six, named </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">oemd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, is a passive backdoor written in C. The backdoor receives the C2 address and port by binding on specific network interfaces. Network interfaces are stored in an environment variable. The backdoor communicates with the C2 over TCP. Communication with C2 is AES-encrypted and XOR-encoded.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The malware configuration is stored in the following environment variables:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">INTFS</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: The network interfaces' names to bind to.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">RTS</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: The routing addresses to bind to (instead of interfaces).</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">UPRT</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: The port to bind to (if not specified, </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">45678</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> is used)</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">DAEMON</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: Run the sample in the background.</span></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">During initialization, malware executes following command to retrieve local-index number of an interface specified in the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">INTFS</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> environment variable:</span></p></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><pre><code>ifinfo &#39;&lt;interface&gt;&#39; | grep local-index | grep -Eo &#39;[0-9]+&#39;</code></pre></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">After setting up a local UDP socket, malware binds to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">0.0.0.0:&lt;port&gt;</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> on the specified interfaces and waits for the attacker to send the C2 address and port. Although the bind socket is a UDP socket, the sample communicates with the C2 using TCP.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Malware supports a set of standard TINYSHELL commands:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">1</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: Upload a file.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">2</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: Download a file.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><code style="vertical-align:baseline">3</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: Execute a shell command.</span></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">When executing shell commands, the malware clears the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">HISTFILE</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> environment variable and allows the attacker to specify the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">TERM</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> value.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Junos OS Specific Socket Options</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">All the previously listed samples create an </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">AF_ROUTE</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> socket using </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">socket(AF_ROUTE(17), SOCK_SEQPACKET(5), 0)</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. Running these samples on a standard FreeBSD system would return an invalid socket, hence we believe this to be a JunosOS specific implementation.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">We believe that this socket is used to establish a connection for communicating with the operating system's routing subsystem. The </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">AF_ROUTE</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> constant designates the socket family for routing operations, and </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">SOCK_SEQPACKET</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> specifies a reliable, message-oriented connection. This socket is used to read and write a packet similar in structure to </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">rt_*</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> messages on OpenBSD to retrieve the interface index. From all the samples, only </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">oemd</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> uses the </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">ifinfo</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> command to retrieve the interface index, while other samples are using a custom </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">rt_*</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> messages via the socket.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The custom message contains an interface name and a logical sub interface. On Juniper routing devices, instead of tagging packets with VLAN IDs, sub-interfaces act as distinct interfaces with their own IP addresses, routing configurations, and potentially different security policies. The interface index value is then passed into a </span><code style="vertical-align:baseline">setsockopt</code><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> call for a command and control socket either TCP or UDP.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Activity in Linux Environments</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant continued to observe UNC3886 leverage similar TTPs and use of the same malware and utilities as detailed in </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/uncovering-unc3886-espionage-operations"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">our previous blog post</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> as follows:</span></p>
<ul>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline"><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Command execution and persistence using a combination of rootkits and utilities, including </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">REPTILE</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">MEDUSA with SEAELF loader</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">, and </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">BUSYBOX</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Instead of using the publicly available kubo/injector as noted previously, Mandiant observed UNC3886 deployed </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">PITHOOK</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> along with a custom SSH server based on the publicly available </span><a href="https://github.com/wzshiming/sshd" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">wzshiming/sshd</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> project to </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/investigating-ivanti-exploitation-persistence/?e=48754805#:~:text=or%20process%20ID.-,PITHOOK,-hooks%20the%20accept"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">hijack</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> SSH authentications and capture SSH credentials.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">TACACS+ daemon binary was replaced by a backdoored version of the binary with similar malicious functions for capturing credentials.</span></p>
</li>
<li style="list-style-type:disc;vertical-align:baseline">
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Use of </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">GHOSTTOWN</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> malware for anti-forensics purposes.</span></p>
</li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant’s investigation did not observe evidence of data staging and exfiltration.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Outlook and Implications</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This blog post further highlights China-nexus espionage actors  are continuing to compromise networking infrastructure with custom malware ecosystems. While UNC3886 previously focused their operations on network edge devices, this activity demonstrated they’re also targeting internal networking infrastructure, such as Internet Service Provider (ISP) routers.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant observed the threat actor targeting network authentication services, including the Terminal Access Controller Access-Control System (TACACS+), and terminal servers with access to the routers to gain privileged initial access.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">This privileged access allowed the threat actor to enter Junos OS shell mode and perform restricted operations. Investigating further actions taken by the threat actor was hampered by the challenges inherent in analyzing proprietary network devices, which required novel methods for artifact acquisition and analysis. </span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant recommends organizations:</span></p>
<ul>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Upgrade Juniper devices and run security checks: </strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Organizations should upgrade their Juniper devices to </span><a href="https://supportportal.juniper.net/JSA93446" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">the latest images</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> which contain mitigations and updated signatures for </span><a href="https://www.juniper.net/documentation/us/en/software/junos/security-services/topics/concept/juniper-malware-removal-tool.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">JMRT</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> and run JMRT Quick Scan and Integrity check after the upgrade. </span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Secure Authentication: </strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Implement a centralized Identity and Access Management (IAM) system with robust multi-factor authentication (MFA) and granular role-based access control (RBAC) for managing network devices.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Configuration Management: </strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Implement a network configuration management that supports configuration validation against defined templates and standards, with the ability to automatically remediate deviations or trigger alerts for manual intervention.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Enhanced Monitoring:</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> Address and prioritize high-risk administrative activities and implement monitoring solutions with a process to regularly review the effectiveness of detection.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Vulnerability Management:</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> Prioritize patching and mitigation of vulnerabilities in network devices, including those in lesser-known operating systems.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Device Lifecycle Management</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline">: Implement a device lifecycle management program that includes proactive monitoring, automated software updates, and end-of-life (EOL) replacement planning to ensure network devices are always supported and secure. </span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Security Hardening:</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> Strengthen the security posture of network devices, administrative devices and systems used for managing network devices by implementing strict access controls, network segmentation, and other security measures.</span></li>
<li><strong style="vertical-align:baseline">Threat Intelligence:</strong><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> Proactively leverage threat intelligence to continually evaluate and improve the effectiveness of security controls against emerging threats.</span></li>
</ul>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">The compromise of routing devices is a recent trend in the tactics of espionage-motivated adversaries as it grants the capability for a long-term, high-level access to the crucial routing infrastructure, with a potential for more disruptive actions in the future. A concerted effort is required to safeguard these critical systems and ensure the continued stability and security of the internet.</span></p>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Organizations potentially impacted by this campaign are strongly advised to engage </span><a href="https://services.google.com/fh/files/misc/custom_threat_hunt_datasheet.pdf" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Mandiant's Custom Threat Hunt service</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">. Mandiant's team of security experts can proactively identify and mitigate hidden threats, providing clarity and confidence in your security posture.</span></p>
<h2><span style="vertical-align:baseline">Indicators of Compromise</span></h2>
<p><span style="vertical-align:baseline">A Google Threat Intelligence Collection of IOCs is <a href="https://www.virustotal.com/gui/collection/69bf6e9649be869b4a0384bd4ce18ca2cad347b60998c31006a881a9f62cd8cc" rel="noopener" target="_blank">available for registered users</a>.</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> For Google Security Operations Enterprise+ customers, rules have been released to your</span><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/chronicle/docs/preview/curated-detections/windows-threats-category"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Emerging Threats</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline"> </span><span style="vertical-align:baseline">rule pack, and indicators of compromise (IOCs) listed in this blog post are available for prioritization with </span><a href="https://cloud.google.com/chronicle/docs/detection"><span style="text-decoration:underline;vertical-align:baseline">Applied Threat Intelligence</span></a><span style="vertical-align:baseline">.</span></p>
